        </a>

       <h1 class="entry-title">
        Even Microsoft Needs Help Avoiding Spreadsheet Errors
       </h1>
       <p class="deck">
        The cells didn't add up in a Microsoft Excel ad posted in the New York subway system. Here's why.
       </p>
       <div class="entry-author">
        <strong>
         <span class="author vcard">
          <a class="url fn" href="http://ww2.cfo.com/author/bill-jelen/" rel="author" title="Posts by Bill Jelen">
           Bill Jelen
          </a>
         </span>
        </strong>
       </div>
       <div class="entry-date">
        <time datetime="November 7, 2013" pubdate="">
         November 7, 2013
        </time>
        | CFO.com | US
       </div>

      <div class="entry-content">
       <p>
        David Yanofsky was riding the New York City subway when he noticed an ad for the new Surface tablet in the Spring Street station. The ad showed a budgeting spreadsheet used in planning a trip to Maui running in Excel 2013 on the Surface tablet. The budget has seven line items, including ones for air, hotel, car, surfing and diving. Unfortunately, the total at the bottom was wrong, as
        <a href="http://ow.ly/qxNK2" title="Check out the second photo">
         Yanofskys photo on Quartz.com shows
        </a>
        .
       </p>
       <p>
        How did it happen?
       </p>
       <p>
        <iframe allowfullscreen="" frameborder="0" height="315" src="//www.youtube.com/embed/O-b8PjQ5_Nc" width="560">
        </iframe>
       </p>
       <div class="related-posts">
        <h5 class="related-posts-heading">
         Recommended Stories:
        </h5>
        <ul>
         <li class="entry-category-spreadsheets">
          <a href="http://ww2.cfo.com/spreadsheets/2015/06/excel-tip-time-formats/">
           Excel Tip: Time Formats
          </a>
         </li>
         <li class="entry-category-spreadsheets">
          <a href="http://ww2.cfo.com/spreadsheets/2015/06/excel-tip-double-trouble/">
           Excel Tip: Double Trouble
          </a>
         </li>
         <li class="entry-category-spreadsheets">
          <a href="http://ww2.cfo.com/spreadsheets/2015/05/excel-tip-find-replace-problems/">
           Excel Tip: Find and Replace Problems
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </div>
       <p>
        If you were creating an Excel worksheet and had to total five numbers, an Excel rookie might use a formula of =B1+B2+B3+B4+B5 as shown here:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <i>
         <a href="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError1.jpg">
          <img alt="ExcelError1" class="size-full wp-image-22746 aligncenter" height="253" src="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError1.jpg" width="478"/>
         </a>
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        Excel pros would instead use the AutoSum button and end up with =SUM(B1:B5).
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <img alt="" class="aligncenter" height="239" width="362"/>
       </p>
       <p>
        Both return an answer of $9000, so the spreadsheet rookie might argue that the original answer is correct and just as good as using the AutoSum button.
       </p>
       <p>
        Although both methods return the same answer, the AutoSum is the right way to do this. Excel has smarts built in that make sure the erroneous answer in the ad will not happen if you use AutoSum. When you insert new rows in the budget, even at the end of the budget, the AutoSum formula will automatically adjust. As soon as you type in a number in B6, the Total formula becomes =SUM(B1:B6)
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <i>
         <a href="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError3.jpg">
          <img alt="ExcelError3" class="size-full wp-image-22748 aligncenter" height="255" src="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError3.jpg" width="359"/>
         </a>
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        Fill in the number in B7 and the total adjusts again:
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <i>
         <a href="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError4.jpg">
          <img alt="ExcelError4" class="size-full wp-image-22749 aligncenter" height="264" src="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError4.jpg" width="347"/>
         </a>
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        These adjustments only happen if you use the SUM version of the formula. If you used the original formula and add new rows, even rows in the middle of the budget, the formula will never adjust, and you will end up with the wrong result shown in the Microsoft ad.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: center;">
        <i>
         <a href="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError5.jpg">
          <img alt="ExcelError5" class="size-full wp-image-22750 aligncenter" height="259" src="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2013/11/ExcelError5.jpg" width="381"/>
         </a>
        </i>
       </p>
       <p>
        Crazy things happen when marketing people try to use Excel. This is just the latest example, one in which Microsofts ad agency produced an interim result that looked OK, but could not adapt.
       </p>
       <p>
        <i>
         Bill Jelen is a contributing editor and the host of MrExcel.com. He points out that his book, Dont Fear the Spreadsheet, warns against this exact type of formula.
        </i>
       </p>
       <div class="ctx-module-container ctx-clearfix" id="ctx-module">
       </div>
      </div>

I have experienced some companies really go nuts with spreadsheets as their logic and functions are inaccurate and eventually giving a flawed answer. However, if you use it accurately then it may be the best tool available in the market by considering its functionality.

             said

            <a class="comment-date" href="http://ww2.cfo.com/spreadsheets/2013/11/even-microsoft-needs-help-avoiding-spreadsheet-errors/#comment-9822">
             11/08/13 10:28am
            </a>
           </div>
           <!-- .comment_author .vcard -->
           <p>
            Even better than summing rows 1 through 5 would be to leave a blank space at the top and bottom of the area to be totaled and include these within the =Sum formula. This allows for added rows, even adding one below the last function.
           </p>
           <p>
            A check sum of some sort is also helpful, whether its percentages which should total 100% or tabulating rows and columns and then checking that the sum of the rows equals the sum of the columns.
           </p>
           <div class="reply">

           <!-- .comment_author .vcard -->
           <p>
            This is a funny story about marketing people, but it does help make a more serious point about checking your final output before it causes reputational damage. For some people, the damage can be real, whether in financial loss or even safety calculations in engineering. The European Spreadsheet Risk Interest Group (EuSpRIG) takes a professional interest in risks in end-user computing and has an annual conference to discuss ways to prevent &amp; detect errors and improve productivity and efficiency in the use of spreadsheets. The next is in Delft in July 2014, see
            <a href="http://www.eusprig.org" rel="nofollow">
             http://www.eusprig.org
            </a>

           <p>
            So typical of non financial people to not 2x check their work.  Real Accountants and Finance people are taught that immediately:  ALWAYS 2x check your work.  This is a easy mistake to make because the formula only adds the cells you tell it to and that doesnt include a series of cells (=sumA3.A21) if that is the result that is desired.  Who the hell cares about a graph if the answer is wrong.

        <aside id="lead-leader" style="background-color:black;color:white;text-align:left;padding: 18px 0;-webkit-border-radius: 10px;-moz-border-radius: 10px;border-radius: 10px;font-family:sans-serif;margin-top:4px;font-size:18px;">
         <h1 style="font-size:25px;background-color:#b22d25;padding:0px 0px 4px 14px;line-height:34px;margin-bottom:10px;">
          <a href="http://performance.cfo.com/resources/expenses-re-examined/?utm_source=CFO&amp;utm_medium=lead-leader-tout&amp;utm_term=expenses&amp;utm_content=tax-liabilities&amp;utm_campaign=expenses-re-examined" style="color:white;font-weight:bold;" title="Expenses Re-Examined.">
           Are your expenses creating value?
          </a>
         </h1>
         <a href="http://performance.cfo.com/resources/expenses-re-examined/?utm_source=CFO&amp;utm_medium=lead-leader-tout&amp;utm_term=expenses&amp;utm_content=tax-liabilities&amp;utm_campaign=expenses-re-examined" style="color:white;font-weight:bold;" title="Considering important tax liabilities.">
          <img alt="There are important tax liabilities to consider." src="http://cdn.cfo.com/content/uploads/2014/08/accounting.jpg" style="height:100px;float:right;margin:6px;margin-right: 24px;" title="Scandalous expense pitfalls that CFOs should avoid."/>
         </a>
         <h2 style="font-size:17px;font-weight:bold; padding-left: 18px;color:white;">
          Balance cost cutting with value creation.
         </h2>
         <p style="padding-left:18px;">
          Learn how to reduce spending and avoid tax liabilities.
         </p>

          Find the best balance between budget and innovative technology.
         </p>
